# TurboQuant: Online Vector Quantization with Near-optimal Distortion Rate

Amir Zandieh  
Google Research

[zandieh@google.com](2504.19874v1/mailto:zandieh@google.com "")      Majid Daliri

New York University  
[daliri.majid@nyu.edu](2504.19874v1/mailto:daliri.majid@nyu.edu "")      Majid Hadian

Google DeepMind  
[majidh@google.com](2504.19874v1/mailto:majidh@google.com "")      Vahab Mirrokni

Google Research  
[mirrokni@google.com](2504.19874v1/mailto:mirrokni@google.com "")  

###### Abstract

Vector quantization, a problem rooted in Shannon’s source coding theory, aims to quantize high-dimensional Euclidean vectors while minimizing distortion in their geometric structure.
We propose TurboQuant to address both mean-squared error (MSE) and inner product distortion, overcoming limitations of existing methods that fail to achieve optimal distortion rates.
Our data-oblivious algorithms, suitable for online applications, achieve near-optimal distortion rates (within a small constant factor) across all bit-widths and dimensions.TurboQuant achieves this by randomly rotating input vectors, inducing a concentrated Beta distribution on coordinates, and leveraging the near-independence property of distinct coordinates in high dimensions to simply apply optimal scalar quantizers per each coordinate.
Recognizing that MSE-optimal quantizers introduce bias in inner product estimation, we propose a two-stage approach: applying an MSE quantizer followed by a 1-bit Quantized JL (QJL) transform on the residual, resulting in an unbiased inner product quantizer.
We also provide a formal proof of the information-theoretic lower bounds on best achievable distortion rate by any vector quantizer, demonstrating that TurboQuant closely matches these bounds, differing only by a small constant (<!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\approx 2.7" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi /><mo>≈</mo><mn>2.7</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\approx 2.7</annotation></semantics></math> --> ≈2.7\approx 2.7) factor.
Experimental results validate our theoretical findings, showing that for KV cache quantization, we achieve absolute quality neutrality with 3.5 bits per channel and marginal quality degradation with 2.5 bits per channel.
Furthermore, in nearest neighbor search tasks, our method outperforms existing product quantization techniques in recall while reducing indexing time to virtually zero.

## 1 Introduction

Vector quantization (VQ) in Euclidean space is crucial for efficiently handling high-dimensional vectors across a spectrum of computational domains, from training and deploying large-scale AI and deep learning models to powering vector databases for search/retrieval systems.
The core objective is to compress high dimensional vectors by quantizing them–converting floating-point coordinate values to low-bitwidth integers–while minimizing distortion, quantified by metrics such as mean-squared error (MSE) or inner product errors.
By preserving these properties, inner product queries can be answered rapidly, with minimal latency, and using reduced computational and communication resources.

This problem’s roots trace back to Shannon’s seminal work on Source Coding theory [[48](#bib.bib48 ""), [49](#bib.bib49 "")], which established that the least distortion achievable by block source codes, now known as vector quantizers, is defined by the Shannon distortion-rate function, determined by the statistical properties of the source and the chosen distortion measure, such as MSE.
Today, VQ plays a critical role in fundamental computational domains, including AI, deep learning, and search systems.

A key application of VQ is in the deployment of AI models, including large language models (LLMs) [[5](#bib.bib5 ""), [18](#bib.bib18 ""), [7](#bib.bib7 ""), [52](#bib.bib52 "")].
As LLM capabilities depend heavily on their model size and context length [[34](#bib.bib34 "")], serving them requires substantial memory demands and increased inference latency.
This latency is primarily attributed to communication bottlenecks between HBM and SRAM on accelerators, or across distributed clusters.
By compressing or quantizing model weights and activations, we can effectively mitigate these bottlenecks, resulting in significant reductions in inference costs.
Inner product operations between activations and weights is at the core of deep learning models.
Thus, model quantization schemes strive to compress weights and/or activation vectors while accurately preserving these inner products.

Decoder based transformer models [[54](#bib.bib54 "")] present another compelling use case.
These models must store key/value (KV) embeddings from previously generated tokens in the KV cache, the size of which scales with both model size (number of layers and attention heads) and context length.
This scaling is a significant bottleneck in terms of memory usage and computational speed, especially for long context models.
Therefore, reducing the KV cache size without compromising accuracy is essential.
In this context, the preservation of the Euclidean structure of these embedding vectors–their inner products and distances–is crucial for maintaining model performance.
VQ emerges as the most suitable framework for addressing this challenge, offering a robust approach to compressing high-dimensional embeddings while preserving their essential geometric properties.

Additionally, nearest neighbor (NN) search in high-dimensional spaces with inner product or cosine similarity [[1](#bib.bib1 ""), [27](#bib.bib27 "")] is a cornerstone of vector databases [[4](#bib.bib4 ""), [2](#bib.bib2 ""), [3](#bib.bib3 "")].
These databases are fundamental for retrieval-augmented generation [[23](#bib.bib23 ""), [19](#bib.bib19 "")] and information retrieval [[35](#bib.bib35 ""), [46](#bib.bib46 "")].
VQ, a.k.a. product quantization (PQ), plays a critical role in these applications.
It enables efficient compression of database vectors, optimizes memory usage, and facilitates low-latency, accurate estimations of inner products with query vectors, thereby enabling fast and precise nearest neighbor searches.

Existing VQ algorithms present a trade-off: either they lack accelerator (vectorization) compatibility and exhibit slow computation, making them unsuitable for real-time AI applications like KV cache quantization, or they suffer from suboptimal distortion bounds relative to bit-width.
Our objective is to introduce an algorithm that addresses these limitations.
Specifically, we design TurboQuant: a lightweight, capable of online application (crucial for scenarios like KV cache quantization), and highly accelerator-friendly—a critical attribute for modern AI workloads.

The core of TurboQuant is a two-stage process.
First, we develop a vector quantizer with optimal distortion rate in terms of mean-squared error (MSE).
Subsequently, we apply a 1-bit quantizer to the residual, resulting in an unbiased and low-distortion inner product quantizer.
We demonstrate that quantizers optimized for MSE do not produce unbiased estimators for inner products, and our two-stage solution effectively bridges this gap.
Our MSE-optimal quantizer starts by randomly rotating <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="d" display="inline" id="S1.p7.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd-dimensional input vectors.
Observing the key fact that each coordinate in the rotated vectors follows a Beta distribution, we design optimal Lloyd-Max quantizer [[42](#bib.bib42 ""), [43](#bib.bib43 "")] for each coordinate by solving a continuous k-means problem.
This method gives optimal MSE distortion bound and minimizes the L2 norm of the residual.
To obtain an unbiased and low-distortion quantizer for inner products, we compose our quantizer with the recently developed Quantized Johnson-Lindenstrauss (QJL) transform [[62](#bib.bib62 "")], which quantizes each coordinate of the residual vector to a single bit.
Our algorithm offers provably optimal distortion bounds for both MSE and inner products, achieving an exponential improvement over existing methods in terms of bit-width dependence.

Crucially, we require an inverse map, <!-- MathML: <math alttext="Q^{-1}:\{0,1\}^{B}\to\mathbb{R}^{d}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS1.p1.8.m8" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msup><mi>Q</mi><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><mo lspace="0.278em" rspace="0.278em">:</mo><mrow><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><mn>0</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mi>B</mi></msup><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q^{-1}:\{0,1\}^{B}\to\mathbb{R}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q−1:{0,1}B→ℝdQ^{-1}:\{0,1\}^{B}\to\mathbb{R}^{d} that performs dequantization, approximately reconstructing original vectors from their quantized representations.
Of course, this transformation is inherently lossy, as <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="Q" id="S1.SS1.p1.9.m9" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>Q</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q</annotation></semantics></math> --> QQ is not a bijection.
So, our primary objective is to minimize distortion, with a specific focus on mean-squared error (MSE) and inner product distortion.

We make no assumptions about the input vector dataset, considering the worst-case scenario.
We let the quantizer <!-- MathML: <math alttext="Q(\cdot)" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS1.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>Q</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">⋅</mo><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q(\cdot)</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q​(⋅)Q(\cdot) to be randomized, leading to stochastic outputs.
Considering randomized quantizers, it is more appropriate to define the expected distortion over the randomness of the quantizer’s output.

### 1.2 Related Work

#### Beginnings of VQ.

The vector quantization theory started by Shannon’s seminal work [[48](#bib.bib48 ""), [49](#bib.bib49 "")] on achievable distortion-rate functions.
In 1963, Zador [[61](#bib.bib61 "")] made significant advances by employing high-resolution methods to derive the limiting operational distortion-rate function for fixed-rate quantization at high rates that closely matches Shannon’s distortion-rate function.
However, Zador did not specifically consider implementable algorithms.
Gersho’s influential paper [[25](#bib.bib25 "")], further advanced the vector quantization by popularizing high-resolution theory, simplifying Zador’s results, introducing lattice vector quantization, and proposing a key conjecture that shaped the field.
Despite these theoretical advancements, the practical applicability of vector quantization remained unclear in early years.
The most straightforward encoding method, brute-force nearest neighbor search, was computationally expensive, hindering the adoption of VQ in practice.

#### Online vs Offline Quantization.

Online (data-oblivious) quantization methods apply instantly without needing data-specific tuning or calibrations [[16](#bib.bib16 ""), [8](#bib.bib8 ""), [41](#bib.bib41 ""), [47](#bib.bib47 ""), [28](#bib.bib28 "")].
In contrast, offline (data-dependent) methods require heavy preprocessing and learning to adapt the quantization map to the data, making them unsuitable for dynamic data scenarios [[37](#bib.bib37 "")].
For instance, methods such as those presented in [[20](#bib.bib20 ""), [39](#bib.bib39 ""), [57](#bib.bib57 ""), [13](#bib.bib13 "")] use second-order (Hessian) information to tune the quantization map which requires heavy preprocessing and even in some cases post processing as well.

#### Online KV Cache Compression.

Several approaches have been proposed to compress the KV cache.
These include architectural modifications [[50](#bib.bib50 ""), [6](#bib.bib6 ""), [15](#bib.bib15 "")] which restructure the transformer to minimize the number of stored key-value pairs.
Additionally, pruning or evicting redundant or less critical tokens has emerged as another approach [[11](#bib.bib11 ""), [66](#bib.bib66 ""), [40](#bib.bib40 ""), [58](#bib.bib58 ""), [64](#bib.bib64 ""), [38](#bib.bib38 ""), [29](#bib.bib29 "")].

A simple yet effective approach to reducing KV cache size is quantizing the KV cache.
Several quantization techniques have been developed specifically for this purpose [[60](#bib.bib60 ""), [59](#bib.bib59 ""), [17](#bib.bib17 ""), [33](#bib.bib33 ""), [65](#bib.bib65 ""), [41](#bib.bib41 ""), [30](#bib.bib30 ""), [36](#bib.bib36 ""), [28](#bib.bib28 "")].
Recently, a new quantization called QJL [[62](#bib.bib62 "")] introduced an efficient, data-oblivious 1-bit quantization approach based on sketching techniques, which provides unbiased estimates for inner product queries.
This method does not require tuning or adaptation to the input data and we make use of this technology in our quantizer optimized for inner product distortion.

#### Product Quantization (PQ).

In Near Neighbor (NN) search problem with Euclidean datasets, the index size poses a significant memory bottleneck, often mitigated by quantization techniques, commonly referred to as Product Quantization (PQ) in the NN literature.
Many of these algorithms rely on constructing a quantization codebook using variations of k-means during the indexing phase [[31](#bib.bib31 ""), [9](#bib.bib9 ""), [24](#bib.bib24 ""), [56](#bib.bib56 ""), [27](#bib.bib27 "")].
Therefore, these methods are ill-suited for online settings due to their requirement for extensive preprocessing.

Recently, a grid-based PQ method was introduced in [[22](#bib.bib22 "")], eliminating the need for preprocessing.
This approach operates by projecting a uniform grid onto the unit sphere and conducting a search to identify the nearest projection to the data points.
While the paper’s theoretical guarantees are suboptimal, likely due to loose analysis—as practical performance surpasses theoretical bounds—the grid projection and binary search algorithm is also computationally slow and particularly inefficient on accelerators like GPU because of their algorithm’s inherent lack of vectorization, which prevents parallel processing.

### 1.3 Overview of Techniques and Contributions

#### MSE Optimzied TurboQuant.

Our first VQ algorithm is designed to minimize MSE distortion deinfed in ??.
To achieve this, we apply a random rotation to the input vectors, thereby inducing a Beta distribution on each coordinate, irrespective of the input vectors themselves.
In high dimensions <!-- MathML: <math alttext="d" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd, the distribution of each coordinate converges to a Gaussian distribution <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathcal{N}(1,1/d)" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒩</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>,</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{N}(1,1/d)</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒩​(1,1/d)\mathcal{N}(1,1/d) due to concentration of measure and the central limit theorem.
Furthermore, any two distinct coordinates become nearly uncorrelated and, more importantly, almost independent (a deeper result that goes beyond just correlation).
This near-independence is a crucial aspect that simplifies our quantization design.
It allows us to quantize each coordinate using optimal scalar quantization, disregarding interactions or correlations between different coordinates, while still achieving near-optimal distortion.

We find optimal scalar quantizers for random variables with Beta distributions by solving a continuous <!-- MathML: <math alttext="1" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mn>1</mn><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1</annotation></semantics></math> --> 11-dimensional k-means problem using the Max-Lloyd algorithm.
We precompute and store these optimal codebooks for a range of practically useful bit-widths, to enable efficient subsequent invocations of our TurboQuant algorithm.

      For small bit-widths the above distortion upper bound can be further refined.

Note that the unit norm assumption, <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\left\|x\right\|_{2}=1" display="inline" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p4.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mrow><mo>‖</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>‖</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\left\|x\right\|_{2}=1</annotation></semantics></math> --> ‖x‖2=1\left\|x\right\|_{2}=1, is standard and not restrictive.
For datasets that do not satisfy this assumption we can compute and store the <!-- MathML: <math alttext="L2" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p4.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>L</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">L2</annotation></semantics></math> --> L​2L2 norms in floating-point precision and rescale the dequantized points using these stored norms.

#### Inner Product TurboQuant.

We show that the MSE optimized quantizers are biased for inner product estimation and thus a different VQ scheme is needed to get an unbiased inner product quantizer.
Our solution is a two stage algorithm that first applies the abovementioned <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="Q_{\tt mse}" display="inline" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚖𝚜𝚎Q_{\tt mse} with a bit-width one less than our target budget and then apply a QJL [[62](#bib.bib62 "")] on the residual error.
This is proved to be unbiased and also has nearly optimal inner product error rate.

#### Lower Bound.

As demonstrated by our lower bounds, TurboQuant’s MSE distortion is provably within a factor of at most <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2}\approx{\bf 2.7}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px3.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mrow><msqrt><mn>3</mn></msqrt><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>π</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></mfrac><mo>≈</mo><mn mathvariant="bold" class="ltx_mathvariant_bold">2.7</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2}\approx{\bf 2.7}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 3​π2≈2.7\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2}\approx{\bf 2.7} of the information-theoretical lower bound.
Notably, for smaller bit-widths, this factor significantly decreases.
For instance, at a bit-width of <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="b=1" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px3.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b=1</annotation></semantics></math> --> b=1b=1 TurboQuant achieves a distortion that is only a factor of approximately <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\bf 1.45}" display="inline" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px3.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mn mathvariant="bold" class="ltx_mathvariant_bold">1.45</mn><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bf 1.45}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 1.45{\bf 1.45} away from the optimal which is also confirmed by our experimental results, indicating its efficiency in low-bit-width scenarios.

#### Experimental Results.

In ??, we empirically validate our theoretical distortion bounds, demonstrating that TurboQuant’s observed distortions closely align with our predictions across various real-world datasets, approaching the established lower bounds.

Furthermore, in ?? and ??, we showcase TurboQuant’s efficacy in online KV cache quantization.
Specifically, we achieve perfect long-context retrieval in needle-in-a-haystack tasks and maintain high performance on other long-context downstream tasks, all while compressing the KV cache by a factor exceeding <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="5\times" intent=":literal" id="S1.SS3.SSS0.Px4.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_math_unparsed"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mo lspace="0.222em">×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\times</annotation></semantics></math> --> 5×5\times.

Finally in ?? we apply TurboQuant to various high-dimensional near neighbor search tasks.TurboQuant consistently outperforms data-dependent product quantization (PQ), while reducing the indexing time to essentially zero.

## 2 Preliminaries

We use boldface lowercase letters, such as <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{x}}" id="S2.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝒙</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒙{\bm{x}} and <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="{\bm{y}}" intent=":literal" id="S2.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝒚</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{y}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒚{\bm{y}}, to denote vectors, and boldface uppercase letters, like <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="{\bm{M}}" id="S2.p1.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝑴</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{M}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝑴{\bm{M}}, to denote matrices.

We use the notation <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\SS^{d-1}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S2.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> SSd−1\SS^{d-1} to denote the hypersphere in <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\mathbb{R}^{d}" display="inline" id="S2.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathbb{R}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> ℝd\mathbb{R}^{d} of radius <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="1" id="S2.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mn>1</mn><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1</annotation></semantics></math> --> 11.

For random variables <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="x" id="S2.p2.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>x</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">x</annotation></semantics></math> --> xx and <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="y" intent=":literal" id="S2.p2.7.m7" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>y</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">y</annotation></semantics></math> --> yy, the mutual information between them is denoted as <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="I(x;y)=h(x)-h(x|y)" display="inline" id="S2.p2.8.m8" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi>I</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>;</mo><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mrow><mi>h</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>−</mo><mrow><mi>h</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>x</mi><mo fence="false">|</mo><mi>y</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">I(x;y)=h(x)-h(x|y)</annotation></semantics></math> --> I​(x;y)=h​(x)−h​(x|y)I(x;y)=h(x)-h(x|y).

Given that TurboQuant employs random rotation to mitigate worst-case input scenarios, understanding the statistical properties of random points on a hypersphere is essential.
The following lemma outlines one such property that we will need for analysis and design purposes:

###### Lemma 1 (coordinate distribution of random point on hypersphere).

For any positive integer <!-- MathML: <math alttext="d" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="Thmlemma1.p1.1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd if <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmlemma1.p1.2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐱</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱∈SSd−1{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1} is a random variable uniformly distributed over the unit hypersphere, then for any <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="j\in[d]" id="Thmlemma1.p1.3.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>j</mi><mo>∈</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><mi>d</mi><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">j\in[d]</annotation></semantics></math> --> j∈[d]j\in[d] the coordinate <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{x}}_{j}" display="inline" id="Thmlemma1.p1.4.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>𝐱</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}_{j}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱j{\bm{x}}_{j} follows the following (scaled/shifted) Beta distribution:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{x}}_{j}\sim f_{X}(x):=\frac{\Gamma(d/2)}{\sqrt{\pi}\cdot\Gamma((d-1)/2)}\left(1-x^{2}\right)^{(d-3)/2}." display="block" id="S2.Ex2.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><msub><mi>𝒙</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><mo>∼</mo><mrow><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>:=</mo><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Γ</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mrow><msqrt><mi>π</mi></msqrt><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Γ</mi></mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mfrac><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><msup><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>−</mo><msup><mi>x</mi><mn>2</mn></msup></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>3</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}_{j}\sim f_{X}(x):=\frac{\Gamma(d/2)}{\sqrt{\pi}\cdot\Gamma((d-1)/2)}\left(1-x^{2}\right)^{(d-3)/2}.</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒙j∼fX​(x):=Γ​(d/2)π⋅Γ​((d−1)/2)​(1−x2)(d−3)/2.{\bm{x}}_{j}\sim f_{X}(x):=\frac{\Gamma(d/2)}{\sqrt{\pi}\cdot\Gamma((d-1)/2)}\left(1-x^{2}\right)^{(d-3)/2}. |  |
| --- | --- | --- |


In high dimensions this beta distribtion converges to the normal distribution <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="f_{X}(\cdot)\to\mathcal{N}(0,1/d)" display="inline" id="Thmlemma1.p1.5.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">⋅</mo><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒩</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>0</mn><mo>,</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">f_{X}(\cdot)\to\mathcal{N}(0,1/d)</annotation></semantics></math> --> fX​(⋅)→𝒩​(0,1/d)f_{X}(\cdot)\to\mathcal{N}(0,1/d).

###### Proof.

<!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="f_{X}(x)" id="S2.1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">f_{X}(x)</annotation></semantics></math> --> fX​(x)f_{X}(x) equals the ratio of the area of a sphere with radius <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\sqrt{1-x^{2}}" display="inline" id="S2.1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msqrt><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>−</mo><msup><mi>x</mi><mn>2</mn></msup></mrow></msqrt><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\sqrt{1-x^{2}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 1−x2\sqrt{1-x^{2}} in dimension <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="d-1" id="S2.1.p1.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d-1</annotation></semantics></math> --> d−1d-1 to the volume of a unit sphere in dimension <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="d" id="S2.1.p1.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd scaled down by <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="1/\sqrt{1-x^{2}}" display="inline" id="S2.1.p1.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><msqrt><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>−</mo><msup><mi>x</mi><mn>2</mn></msup></mrow></msqrt></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1/\sqrt{1-x^{2}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 1/1−x21/\sqrt{1-x^{2}} (by Pythagorean theorem).
Therefore,

|  | <!-- MathML: <math display="block" intent=":literal" alttext="f_{X}(x)=\frac{\frac{2\pi^{(d-1)/2}}{\Gamma((d-1)/2)}\cdot(1-x^{2})^{(d-2)/2}}{\frac{2\pi^{d/2}}{\Gamma(d/2)}}\cdot | --- | --- | --- |


∎

### 2.1 Shannon Lower Bound on Distortion

The Shannon Lower Bound (SLB) is a powerful tool, derived from Shannon’s lossy source coding theorem [[49](#bib.bib49 "")], that provides a universal lower bound on the optimal achievable distortion rate for any lossy compression scheme.
Specifically, we use a version of SLB tailored for the mean-squared error (MSE) distortion measure applied to general <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="d" id="S2.SS1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd-dimensional sources.

###### Lemma 2 (SLB).

Let <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{x}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}" display="inline" id="Thmlemma2.p1.1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐱</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱∈ℝd{\bm{x}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d} be a random vector with an arbitrary probability distribution <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="p_{X}" id="Thmlemma2.p1.2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>p</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">p_{X}</annotation></semantics></math> --> pXp_{X} and finite differential entropy <!-- MathML: <math alttext="h({\bm{x}})" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmlemma2.p1.3.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>h</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝐱</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">h({\bm{x}})</annotation></semantics></math> --> h​(𝐱)h({\bm{x}}).
Define the MSE distortion-rate function <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="D(B)" id="Thmlemma2.p1.4.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>D</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>B</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D(B)</annotation></semantics></math> --> D​(B)D(B) for total bit complexity <!-- MathML: <math alttext="B\geq 0" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmlemma2.p1.5.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>B</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">B\geq 0</annotation></semantics></math> --> B≥0B\geq 0 as:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math display="block" intent=":literal" alttext="D(p_{X},B):=\inf\left\{\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left\|{\bm{x}}-{\bm{y}}\right\|_{2}^{2}\right]:I({\bm{x}};{\bm{y}})\leq B\right\}," id="S2.Ex4.m1" class="ltx_math_unparsed"><semantics><mrow><mi>D</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>p</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>B</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo rspace="0.1389em">:=</mo><mo lspace="0.1389em" movablelimits="false" rspace="0em">inf</mo><mrow><mo>{</mo><mo movablelimits="false" rspace="0em" lspace="0em">𝔼</mo><mrow><mo>[</mo><mo rspace="0.167em" stretchy="true" lspace="0em">∥</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>−</mo><mi>𝒚</mi><msubsup><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0.167em" stretchy="true">∥</mo><mn>2</mn><mn>2</mn></msubsup><mo rspace="0.278em">]</mo></mrow><mo rspace="0.278em">:</mo><mi>I</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>;</mo><mi>𝒚</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>≤</mo><mi>B</mi><mo>}</mo></mrow><mo>,</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D(p_{X},B):=\inf\left\{\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left\|{\bm{x}}-{\bm{y}}\right\|_{2}^{2}\right]:I({\bm{x}};{\bm{y}})\leq B\right\},</annotation></semantics></math> --> D(pX,B):=inf{𝔼[∥𝒙−𝒚∥22]:I(𝒙;𝒚)≤B},D(p_{X},B):=\inf\left\{\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left\|{\bm{x}}-{\bm{y}}\right\|_{2}^{2}\right]:I({\bm{x}};{\bm{y}})\leq B\right\}, |  |
| --- | --- | --- |


where the infimum is taken over all joint distributions of <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\bm{x}}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmlemma2.p1.6.1.m1" id="Thmlemma2.p1.9.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>B</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">B</annotation></semantics></math> --> BB and <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left\|{\bm{x}}-{\bm{y}}\right\|_{2}^{2}\right]" display="inline" id="Thmlemma2.p1.10.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mo Then, for any bit complexity <!-- MathML: <math alttext="B\geq 0" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmlemma2.p1.13.8.m8" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>B</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">B\geq 0</annotation></semantics></math> --> B≥0B\geq 0, the following Shannon Lower Bound holds:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="block" alttext="D(p_{X},B)\geq\frac{d}{2\pi e}\cdot 2^{(2/d)(h({\bm{x}})-B)}." id="S2.Ex5.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mi>D</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>p</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>B</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>≥</mo><mrow><mfrac><mi>d</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>π</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>e</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><msup><mn>2</mn><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mrow><mi>h</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>−</mo><mi>B</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></msup></mrow></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D(p_{X},B)\geq\frac{d}{2\pi e}\cdot 2^{(2/d)(h({\bm{x}})-B)}.</annotation></semantics></math> --> D​(pX,B)≥d2​π​e⋅2(2/d)​(h​(𝒙)−B).D(p_{X},B)\geq\frac{d}{2\pi e}\cdot 2^{(2/d)(h({\bm{x}})-B)}. |  |
| --- | --- | --- |

This is a classic result proved using backward Gaussian test channel (for a proof see [[14](#bib.bib14 "")]).
Our lower bound result uses a corollary of SLB that corresponds to the uniformly distributed random points on the unit hyeprsphere.
We present this in the following lemma:

###### Lemma 3 (SLB for random point on hypersphere).

Let <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}" display="inline" id="Thmlemma3.p1.1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐱</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱∈SSd−1{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1} be a random variable uniformly distributed over the unit hypersphere and define the MSE distortion-rate function <!-- MathML: <math alttext="D(B)" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmlemma3.p1.2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>D</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>B</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D(B)</annotation></semantics></math> --> D​(B)D(B) for total bit complexity <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="B" id="Thmlemma3.p1.3.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>B</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">B</annotation></semantics></math> --> BB as per ??.
Then, for any bit complexity <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="B\geq 0" intent=":literal" id="Thmlemma3.p1.4.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>B</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">B\geq 0</annotation></semantics></math> --> B≥0B\geq 0, the following distortion lower bound holds:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math alttext="D(B)\geq 2^{-2B/d}." intent=":literal" display="block" id="S2.Ex6.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mi>D</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>B</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>≥</mo><msup><mn>2</mn><mrow><mo>−</mo><mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>B</mi></mrow><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></mrow></msup></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D(B)\geq 2^{-2B/d}.</annotation></semantics></math> --> D​(B)≥2−2​B/d.D(B)\geq 2^{-2B/d}. |  |
| --- | --- | --- |

###### Proof.

If we let <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="A_{d}" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>A</mi><mi>d</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">A_{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> AdA_{d} denote the area of the hypersphere <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\SS^{d-1}" id="S2.SS1.1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> SSd−1\SS^{d-1}, the entropy of uniform distribution over hypersphere is <!-- MathML: <math alttext="h({\bm{x}})=\log_{2}A_{d}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S2.SS1.1.p1.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi>h</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><msub><mi>log</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo lspace="0.167em">⁡</mo><msub><mi>A</mi><mi>d</mi></msub></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">h({\bm{x}})=\log_{2}A_{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> h​(𝒙)=log2⁡Adh({\bm{x}})=\log_{2}A_{d}.
Plugging this into the SLB from ?? we get <!-- MathML: <math alttext="D(B)\geq\frac{d}{2\pi e}\cdot{A_{d}}^{2/d}\cdot 2^{-2B/d}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S2.SS1.1.p1.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi>D</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>B</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>≥</mo><mrow><mfrac><mi>d</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>π</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>e</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mmultiscripts><mi>A</mi><mi>d</mi><mrow /><mrow /><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></mmultiscripts><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><msup><mn>2</mn><mrow><mo>−</mo><mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>B</mi></mrow><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></mrow></msup></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D(B)\geq\frac{d}{2\pi e}\cdot{A_{d}}^{2/d}\cdot 2^{-2B/d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D​(B)≥d2​π​e⋅Ad2/d⋅2−2​B/dD(B)\geq\frac{d}{2\pi e}\cdot{A_{d}}^{2/d}\cdot 2^{-2B/d}.
Using Stirling’s approximation formula for Gamma function we have <!-- MathML: <math alttext="A_{d}=\frac{2\pi^{d/2}}{\Gamma(d/2)}\geq\left(\frac{2\pi e}{d}\right)^{d/2}\cdot\sqrt{\frac{2d}{\pi}}\cdot(1-O(1/d))" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S2.SS1.1.p1.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>A</mi><mi>d</mi></msub><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><msup><mi>π</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Γ</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mfrac><mo>≥</mo><mrow><msup><mrow><mo>(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>π</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>e</mi></mrow><mi>d</mi></mfrac><mo rspace="0.055em">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><msqrt><mfrac><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow><mi>π</mi></mfrac></msqrt><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>−</mo><mrow><mi>O</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">A_{d}=\frac{2\pi^{d/2}}{\Gamma(d/2)}\geq\left(\frac{2\pi e}{d}\right)^{d/2}\cdot\sqrt{\frac{2d}{\pi}}\cdot(1-O(1/d))</annotation></semantics></math> --> Ad=2​πd/2Γ​(d/2)≥(2​π​ed)d/2⋅2​dπ⋅(1−O​(1/d))A_{d}=\frac{2\pi^{d/2}}{\Gamma(d/2)}\geq\left(\frac{2\pi e}{d}\right)^{d/2}\cdot\sqrt{\frac{2d}{\pi}}\cdot(1-O(1/d)).
By substituting this into the inequality obtained from ?? we get the desired lower bound.
∎

### 2.2 QJL: 1-bit inner product quantization

As previously stated, we design two VQ algorithms: one optimized for minimizing MSE and the other for minimizing inner product error.
We show that MSE-optimal quantizers do not necessarily provide unbiased inner product estimates, particularly exhibiting significant bias at lower bit-widths.
Our solution for inner product quantization is a two-stage algorithm.
First, we apply the MSE-optimal quantizer using one less bit than the desired bit-width budget, thus minimizing the L2 norm of the residuals.
Next we apply an unbiased and optimal single-bit quantizer to the residual.
For the single-bit inner product quantizer, we utilize the recently proposed Quantized Johnson-Lindenstrauss (QJL) algorithm [[62](#bib.bib62 "")], which is an optimal inner product quantizer with a bit-width of one. Here, we present the QJL algorithm and its essential theoretical guarantees.

###### Definition 1 (QJL).

For any positive integer <!-- MathML: <math alttext="d" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmdefinition1.p1.1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd the QJL map <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="Q_{\tt qjl}:\mathbb{R}^{d}\to\{-1,+1\}^{d}" intent=":literal" id="Thmdefinition1.p1.2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi></msub><mo lspace="0.278em" rspace="0.278em">:</mo><mrow><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo>,</mo><mrow><mo>+</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt qjl}:\mathbb{R}^{d}\to\{-1,+1\}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚚𝚓𝚕:ℝd→{−1,+1}dQ_{\tt qjl}:\mathbb{R}^{d}\to\{-1,+1\}^{d} is defined as:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math display="block" alttext="Q_{\tt qjl}({\bm{x}}):={\tt sign}\left({\bm{S}}\cdot{\bm{x}}\right)~~~~\text{ for any }{\bm{x}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}," intent=":literal" id="S2.Ex7.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mrow><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>:=</mo><mrow><mi>𝚜𝚒𝚐𝚗</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mi>𝑺</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>𝒙</mi></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><mspace style="width:1.32em;" width="1.32em" /><mrow><mrow><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_italic">&nbsp;for any&nbsp;</mtext><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>𝒙</mi></mrow><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow></mrow><mo>,</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt qjl}({\bm{x}}):={\tt sign}\left({\bm{S}}\cdot{\bm{x}}\right)~~~~\text{ for any }{\bm{x}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d},</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚚𝚓𝚕​(𝒙):=𝚜𝚒𝚐𝚗​(𝑺⋅𝒙) for any ​𝒙∈ℝd,Q_{\tt qjl}({\bm{x}}):={\tt sign}\left({\bm{S}}\cdot{\bm{x}}\right)~~~~\text{ for any }{\bm{x}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}, |  |

where <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="{\bm{S}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}" intent=":literal" id="Thmdefinition1.p1.3.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐒</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">×</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{S}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐒∈ℝd×d{\bm{S}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times d} is a random matrix with i.i.d. entries sampled from the normal distribution <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathcal{N}(0,1)" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmdefinition1.p1.4.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒩</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>0</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{N}(0,1)</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒩​(0,1)\mathcal{N}(0,1) and the <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="{\tt sign}" id="Thmdefinition1.p1.5.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝚜𝚒𝚐𝚗</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\tt sign}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚜𝚒𝚐𝚗{\tt sign} function is applied entry-wise to its vector input.

###### Lemma 4 (performance guarantee: QJL).

Let <!-- MathML: <math alttext="Q_{\tt qjl}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="Thmlemma4.p1.1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt qjl}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚚𝚓𝚕Q_{\tt qjl} and <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="Q_{\tt qjl}^{-1}" display="inline" id="Thmlemma4.p1.2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msubsup><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msubsup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt qjl}^{-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚚𝚓𝚕−1Q_{\tt qjl}^{-1} be defined as per ??.

###### Proof.

The unbiasedness immediately follows from Lemma 3.2 of [[62](#bib.bib62 "")].
To show the variance bound let <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\bm{s}}_{1},{\bm{s}}_{2},\ldots{\bm{s}}_{m}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>𝒔</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>𝒔</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>,</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">…</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><msub><mi>𝒔</mi><mi>m</mi></msub></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{s}}_{1},{\bm{s}}_{2},\ldots{\bm{s}}_{m}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒔1,𝒔2,…​𝒔m{\bm{s}}_{1},{\bm{s}}_{2},\ldots{\bm{s}}_{m} denote the rows of the random matrix <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{S}}" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝑺</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{S}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝑺{\bm{S}} in ??. We have:

∎

## 3 TurboQuant: High Performance Quantization

We developed two VQ algorithms, each tailored to a specific objective.
The first algorithm is designed to minimize the MSE between the original and reconstructed vectors after quantization.
The second algorithm is optimized for unbiased inner product estimation, addressing the bias inherent in MSE-optimal quantizers.
These algorithms are detailed in the following subsections.

Furthermore, in ??, we establish information-theoretic lower bounds on the best achievable distortion rates for any vector quantizer.
This analysis demonstrates that TurboQuant achieve near-optimality, differing from the lower bound by only a small constant factor across all bit-widths.

### 3.1 MSE Optimal TurboQuant

Let <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒙∈SSd−1{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1} be a (worst-case) vector on the unit sphere in dimension <!-- MathML: <math alttext="d" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd.
We aim to quantize <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\bm{x}}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p1.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝒙</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒙{\bm{x}} to <!-- MathML: <math alttext="b" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.p1.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>b</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b</annotation></semantics></math> --> bb bits per coordinate while minimizing the reconstruction MSE defined in ??.
We start by randomizing this vector by multiplying it with a random rotation matrix <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\bm{\Pi}\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p1.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚷</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">×</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bm{\Pi}\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚷∈ℝd×d\bm{\Pi}\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}.
We can generate <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\bm{\Pi}" id="S3.SS1.p1.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝚷</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bm{\Pi}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚷\bm{\Pi} by applying QR decomposition on a random matrix with i.i.d Normal entries.

The resulting rotated vector, <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}" id="S3.SS1.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚷</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>𝒙</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚷⋅𝒙\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}, is uniformly distributed on the unit sphere <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\SS^{d-1}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> SSd−1\SS^{d-1}.
As shown in ??, each coordinate of <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}" id="S3.SS1.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚷</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>𝒙</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚷⋅𝒙\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}} follows a Beta distribution, which converges to a normal distribution in high dimensions.
Furthermore, in high dimensions, distinct coordinates of <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p2.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚷</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>𝒙</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚷⋅𝒙\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}} become nearly independent [[55](#bib.bib55 "")], allowing us to apply optimal scalar quantizers to each coordinate independently.
Therefore, by ??, our task reduces to designing a scalar quantizer for random variables with the distribution <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="f_{X}(x)=\frac{\Gamma(d/2)}{\sqrt{\pi}\cdot\Gamma((d-1)/2)}\left(1-x^{2}\right)^{(d-3)/2}" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.p2.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Γ</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mrow><msqrt><mi>π</mi></msqrt><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Γ</mi></mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mfrac><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><msup><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>−</mo><msup><mi>x</mi><mn>2</mn></msup></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>3</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">f_{X}(x)=\frac{\Gamma(d/2)}{\sqrt{\pi}\cdot\Gamma((d-1)/2)}\left(1-x^{2}\right)^{(d-3)/2}</annotation></semantics></math> --> fX​(x)=Γ​(d/2)π⋅Γ​((d−1)/2)​(1−x2)(d−3)/2f_{X}(x)=\frac{\Gamma(d/2)}{\sqrt{\pi}\cdot\Gamma((d-1)/2)}\left(1-x^{2}\right)^{(d-3)/2} for <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="x\in[-1,1]" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p2.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>x</mi><mo>∈</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo>,</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">x\in[-1,1]</annotation></semantics></math> --> x∈[−1,1]x\in[-1,1].

The optimal scalar quantization problem, given a known probability distribution, can be framed as a continuous k-means problem in dimension one.
Specifically, we aim to partition the interval <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="[-1,1]" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p3.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo>,</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">[-1,1]</annotation></semantics></math> --> [−1,1][-1,1] into <!-- MathML: <math alttext="2^{b}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.p3.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msup><mn>2</mn><mi>b</mi></msup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">2^{b}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 2b2^{b} clusters/buckets.
The optimal solution adheres to a Voronoi tessellation [[42](#bib.bib42 "")], meaning interval boundaries are the midpoints between consecutive centroids, when arranged in sorted order.
Therefore, with <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="c_{i}" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.p3.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>c</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">c_{i}</annotation></semantics></math> --> cic_{i}’s denoting the centroids in ascending order, we can formulate the scalar quantization as the following k-means optimization problem:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b):=\min_{-1\leq c_{1}\leq c_{2}\leq\ldots\leq c_{2^{b}}\leq class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒞</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>b</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>:=</mo><mrow><munder><mi>min</mi><mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo>≤</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mo>≤</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>≤</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">…</mi><mo>≤</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><msup><mn>2</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></msub><mo>≤</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></munder><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><munderover><mo movablelimits="false" rspace="0em">∑</mo><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><msup><mn>2</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></munderover><mrow><msubsup><mo rspace="0em">∫</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mi>i</mi></msub></mrow><mn>2</mn></mfrac><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>c</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub></mrow><mn>2</mn></mfrac></msubsup><mrow><mrow><msup><mrow><mo Note that <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)" id="S3.SS1.p3.4.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒞</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>b</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒞​(fX,b)\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b) in ?? denotes the optimal MSE cost function for bit-width <!-- MathML: <math alttext="b" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p3.5.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>b</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b</annotation></semantics></math> --> bb, a quantity we will bound to prove the upper bound on the end-to-end MSE of TurboQuant.
The problem in ?? can be solved using iterative numerical methods to achieve any desired precision.
We solve ?? for a range of practically relevant bit-widths <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="b" id="S3.SS1.p3.6.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>b</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b</annotation></semantics></math> --> bb once, and store the results for future uses by the quantizer.

Therefore the quantizer <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="Q_{\tt mse}:\mathbb{R}^{d}\to\{0,1\}^{b\cdot d}" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p5.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo lspace="0.278em" rspace="0.278em">:</mo><mrow><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><mn>0</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></msup></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt mse}:\mathbb{R}^{d}\to\{0,1\}^{b\cdot d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚖𝚜𝚎:ℝd→{0,1}b⋅dQ_{\tt mse}:\mathbb{R}^{d}\to\{0,1\}^{b\cdot d} first computes <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p5.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚷</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>𝒙</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚷⋅𝒙\bm{\Pi}\cdot{\bm{x}} and then computes and stores the indices of the nearest centroids to each coordinate of this vector.
The dequantization map <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="Q_{\tt mse}^{-1}:\{0,1\}^{b\cdot d}\to\mathbb{R}^{d}" id="S3.SS1.p5.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msubsup><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msubsup><mo lspace="0.278em" rspace="0.278em">:</mo><mrow><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><mn>0</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt mse}^{-1}:\{0,1\}^{b\cdot d}\to\mathbb{R}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚖𝚜𝚎−1:{0,1}b⋅d→ℝdQ_{\tt mse}^{-1}:\{0,1\}^{b\cdot d}\to\mathbb{R}^{d} reconstructs the vector by retrieving the centroids corresponding to the stored indices and then rotating the result back to the original basis through multiplication with <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\bm{\Pi}^{\top}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p5.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msup><mi>𝚷</mi><mo>⊤</mo></msup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bm{\Pi}^{\top}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚷⊤\bm{\Pi}^{\top}.
A pseudocode for these procedures is given in ??.

We are now ready to prove our main theorem for <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}" id="S3.SS1.p6.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}.

###### Theorem 1 (performance guarantee: <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}).

For any bit-width <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="b\geq 1" id="Thmtheorem1.p1.1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b\geq 1</annotation></semantics></math> --> b≥1b\geq 1 and any vector <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}" id="Thmtheorem1.p1.2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐱</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱∈SSd−1{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}, the procedure <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="\textsc{Quant}_{\tt mse}({\bm{x}})" intent=":literal" id="Thmtheorem1.p1.3.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">Quant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝐱</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{Quant}_{\tt mse}({\bm{x}})</annotation></semantics></math> --> Quant𝚖𝚜𝚎​(𝐱)\textsc{Quant}_{\tt mse}({\bm{x}}) in ?? outputs an index vector <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\tt idx}\in[2^{b}]^{d}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem1.p1.4.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚒𝚍𝚡</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><msup><mn>2</mn><mi>b</mi></msup><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\tt idx}\in[2^{b}]^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚒𝚍𝚡∈[2b]d{\tt idx}\in[2^{b}]^{d}.
When this index vector is passed to the primitive <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{DeQuant}_{\tt mse}({\tt idx})" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem1.p1.5.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">DeQuant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝚒𝚍𝚡</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{DeQuant}_{\tt mse}({\tt idx})</annotation></semantics></math> --> DeQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎​(𝚒𝚍𝚡)\textsc{DeQuant}_{\tt mse}({\tt idx}), it produces a reconstructed vector <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem1.p1.6.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mover accent="true"><mi>𝐱</mi><mo>~</mo></mover><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱~∈ℝd\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d} that satisfies the following distortion bounds:

- •
      MSE defined as <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="D_{\tt mse}:=\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}_{\tilde{{\bm{x}}}}[\left\|{\bm{x}}-\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\right\|_{2}^{2}]" intent=":literal" id="S3.I1.i1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo rspace="0.1389em">:=</mo><mrow><msub><mo lspace="0.1389em" rspace="0em">𝔼</mo><mover accent="true"><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>~</mo></mover></msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><msubsup><mrow><mo>‖</mo><mrow><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>−</mo><mover accent="true"><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>~</mo></mover></mrow><mo>‖</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn><mn>2</mn></msubsup><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}:=\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}_{\tilde{{\bm{x}}}}[\left\|{\bm{x}}-\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\right\|_{2}^{2}]</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎:=𝔼𝒙~[‖𝒙−𝒙~‖22]D_{\tt mse}:=\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}_{\tilde{{\bm{x}}}}[\left\|{\bm{x}}-\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\right\|_{2}^{2}] is bounded by <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="D_{\tt mse}\leq\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}" id="S3.I1.i1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo>≤</mo><mrow><mfrac><mrow><msqrt><mn>3</mn></msqrt><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>π</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mfrac><mn>1</mn><msup><mn>4</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></mfrac></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}\leq\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎≤3​π2⋅14bD_{\tt mse}\leq\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}} for any <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="b\geq 0" display="inline" id="S3.I1.i1.p1.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b\geq 0</annotation></semantics></math> --> b≥0b\geq 0.

- •
      For small bit-widths, specifically <!-- MathML: <math alttext="b=1,2,3,4" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.I1.i2.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>=</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>2</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>3</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>4</mn></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b=1,2,3,4</annotation></semantics></math> --> b=1,2,3,4b=1,2,3,4 the MSE exhibits finer-grained distortion values: <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="D_{\tt mse}\approx{\bf 0.36},{\bf 0.117},{\bf 0.03},{\bf 0.009}" intent=":literal" id="S3.I1.i2.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo>≈</mo><mrow><mn mathvariant="bold" class="ltx_mathvariant_bold">0.36</mn><mo>,</mo><mn mathvariant="bold" class="ltx_mathvariant_bold">0.117</mn><mo>,</mo><mn mathvariant="bold" class="ltx_mathvariant_bold">0.03</mn><mo>,</mo><mn mathvariant="bold" class="ltx_mathvariant_bold">0.009</mn></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}\approx{\bf 0.36},{\bf 0.117},{\bf 0.03},{\bf 0.009}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎≈0.36,0.117,0.03,0.009D_{\tt mse}\approx{\bf 0.36},{\bf 0.117},{\bf 0.03},{\bf 0.009}, respectively.

###### Proof.

We start the proof by showing that <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="D_{\tt mse}=d\cdot\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)" id="S3.SS1.1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo>=</mo><mrow><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒞</mi></mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>b</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}=d\cdot\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎=d⋅𝒞​(fX,b)D_{\tt mse}=d\cdot\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b), where <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒞</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>b</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒞​(fX,b)\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b) is the optimal MSE cost for scalar quantizer defined in ??.
