The last two lines above follows because <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="c_{{\tt idx}_{j}}" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.1.p1.11.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>c</mi><msub><mi>𝚒𝚍𝚡</mi><mi>j</mi></msub></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">c_{{\tt idx}_{j}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> c𝚒𝚍𝚡jc_{{\tt idx}_{j}} is chosen to be the nearest centroid to each coordinate <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="{\bm{y}}_{j}" id="S3.SS1.1.p1.12.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>𝒚</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{y}}_{j}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒚j{\bm{y}}_{j} in line [6](#alg1.l6 "In Algorithm 1 ‣ 3.1 MSE Optimal TurboQuant ‣ 3 TurboQuant: High Performance Quantization ‣ TurboQuant: Online Vector Quantization with Near-optimal Distortion Rate").

Now we must bound the optimal k-means cost <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)" id="S3.SS1.2.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒞</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>b</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒞​(fX,b)\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b).
For moderate values of <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="d" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.2.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd, <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="f_{X}\to\mathcal{N}(0,1/d)" id="S3.SS1.2.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒩</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>0</mn><mo>,</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">f_{X}\to\mathcal{N}(0,1/d)</annotation></semantics></math> --> fX→𝒩​(0,1/d)f_{X}\to\mathcal{N}(0,1/d).
By numerically solving the optimization problem in ?? for values <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="b=1,2,3,4" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.2.p2.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>=</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>2</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>3</mn><mo>,</mo><mn>4</mn></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b=1,2,3,4</annotation></semantics></math> --> b=1,2,3,4b=1,2,3,4 we get that <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)\approx\frac{0.36}{d},\frac{0.117}{d},\frac{0.03}{d},\frac{0.009}{d}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.2.p2.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒞</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>b</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>≈</mo><mrow><mfrac><mn>0.36</mn><mi>d</mi></mfrac><mo>,</mo><mfrac><mn>0.117</mn><mi>d</mi></mfrac><mo>,</mo><mfrac><mn>0.03</mn><mi>d</mi></mfrac><mo>,</mo><mfrac><mn>0.009</mn><mi>d</mi></mfrac></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)\approx\frac{0.36}{d},\frac{0.117}{d},\frac{0.03}{d},\frac{0.009}{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒞​(fX,b)≈0.36d,0.117d,0.03d,0.009d\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)\approx\frac{0.36}{d},\frac{0.117}{d},\frac{0.03}{d},\frac{0.009}{d}, respectively.
For larger bit-widths <!-- MathML: <math alttext="b&gt;4" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.2.p2.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>&gt;</mo><mn>4</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b&gt;4</annotation></semantics></math> --> b>4b>4, we can apply the Panter-Dite [[44](#bib.bib44 "")] high-resolution formula for the distortion of a fixed-rate scalar quantizer, yielding the following bound:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)\leq\frac{1}{12}\cdot\left(\int f_{X}(x)^{1/3}~dx\right)^{3}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}=\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2d}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}." display="block" intent=":literal" id="S3.Ex17.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒞</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>b</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>≤</mo><mrow><mfrac><mn>1</mn><mn>12</mn></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><msup><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mo lspace="0em">∫</mo><mrow><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>3</mn></mrow></msup><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo rspace="0em">𝑑</mo><mi>x</mi></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo rspace="0.055em">)</mo></mrow><mn>3</mn></msup><mo rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mfrac><mn>1</mn><msup><mn>4</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></mfrac></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mfrac><mrow><msqrt><mn>3</mn></msqrt><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>π</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mfrac><mn>1</mn><msup><mn>4</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></mfrac></mrow></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)\leq\frac{1}{12}\cdot\left(\int f_{X}(x)^{1/3}~dx\right)^{3}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}=\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2d}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}.</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒞​(fX,b)≤112⋅(∫fX​(x)1/3​𝑑x)3⋅14b=3​π2​d⋅14b.\mathcal{C}(f_{X},b)\leq\frac{1}{12}\cdot\left(\int f_{X}(x)^{1/3}~dx\right)^{3}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}=\frac{\sqrt{3}\pi}{2d}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}. |  |
| --- | --- | --- |


This completes the proof.
∎

#### Entropy Encoding Codebook Pointers.

TurboQuant’s efficiency can be further increased by applying entropy encoding to the indices that point to the closest codebook elements.
Specifically, the probability of each codeword index appearing in the quantized vectors can be computed as <!-- MathML: <math alttext="p_{\ell}:=\int_{\frac{c_{\ell-1}+c_{\ell}}{2}}^{\frac{c_{\ell}+c_{\ell+1}}{2}}f_{X}(x)~dx" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>p</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">ℓ</mi></msub><mo rspace="0.111em">:=</mo><mrow><msubsup><mo>∫</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">ℓ</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">ℓ</mi></msub></mrow><mn>2</mn></mfrac><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>c</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">ℓ</mi></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">ℓ</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub></mrow><mn>2</mn></mfrac></msubsup><mrow><msub><mi>f</mi><mi>X</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo lspace="0.330em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo rspace="0em">𝑑</mo><mi>x</mi></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">p_{\ell}:=\int_{\frac{c_{\ell-1}+c_{\ell}}{2}}^{\frac{c_{\ell}+c_{\ell+1}}{2}}f_{X}(x)~dx</annotation></semantics></math> --> pℓ:=∫cℓ−1+cℓ2cℓ+cℓ+12fX​(x)​𝑑xp_{\ell}:=\int_{\frac{c_{\ell-1}+c_{\ell}}{2}}^{\frac{c_{\ell}+c_{\ell+1}}{2}}f_{X}(x)~dx.
Optimally coding the indices, reduces the average bit-width to nearly the entropy of the distribution <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\{p_{i}\}_{i\in[2^{b}]}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><msub><mi>p</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>∈</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><msup><mn>2</mn><mi>b</mi></msup><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow></mrow></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\{p_{i}\}_{i\in[2^{b}]}</annotation></semantics></math> --> {pi}i∈[2b]\{p_{i}\}_{i\in[2^{b}]}.
This lossless compression does not affect the distortion and provides a bit-width reduction at no cost.
The most significant reduction occurs for <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="b=4" id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>4</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b=4</annotation></semantics></math> --> b=4b=4, where the entropy of <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="\{p_{i}\}_{i\in[2^{b}]}" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><msub><mi>p</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>∈</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><msup><mn>2</mn><mi>b</mi></msup><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow></mrow></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\{p_{i}\}_{i\in[2^{b}]}</annotation></semantics></math> --> {pi}i∈[2b]\{p_{i}\}_{i\in[2^{b}]} is approximately <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="3.8" id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mn>3.8</mn><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">3.8</annotation></semantics></math> --> 3.83.8.
Detailed calculations for optimal prefix codes reveal that the average bit-width can be reduced by <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="5\%" id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mo>%</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\%</annotation></semantics></math> --> 5%5\%.
However, given the limited gain, we have chosen not to incorporate this technique into TurboQuant to maintain simplicity and speed.

### 3.2 Inner-product Optimal TurboQuant

For important applications like nearest neighbor search, having an unbiased inner product estimator is essential.
However, <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}" display="inline" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse} presented in ?? does not provide unbiased inner product estimates with query vectors.
To illustrate this, consider the case with a bit-width of <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="b=1" display="inline" id="S3.SS2.p1.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b=1</annotation></semantics></math> --> b=1b=1.
In this scenario, the optimal codebooks that solve the optimization problem in ??, for sufficiently large <!-- MathML: <math alttext="d" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.p1.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd, are <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\left\{\pm\sqrt{\frac{2}{\pi d}}\right\}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S3.SS2.p1.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mo>{</mo><mrow><mo>±</mo><msqrt><mfrac><mn>2</mn><mrow><mi>π</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>d</mi></mrow></mfrac></msqrt></mrow><mo>}</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\left\{\pm\sqrt{\frac{2}{\pi d}}\right\}</annotation></semantics></math> --> {±2π​d}\left\{\pm\sqrt{\frac{2}{\pi d}}\right\}.

Therefore, for large enough <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="d" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.p1.10.m10" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>d</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">d</annotation></semantics></math> --> dd, according to ??, we have <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left&lt;{\bm{y}},Q_{\tt mse}^{-1}\left(Q_{\tt mse}({\bm{x}})\right)\right&gt;\right]=\frac{2}{\pi}\cdot\langle{\bm{y}},{\bm{x}}\rangle" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.p1.11.m11" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mo rspace="0em">𝔼</mo><mrow><mo>[</mo><mrow><mo>⟨</mo><mi>𝒚</mi><mo>,</mo><mrow><msubsup><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msubsup><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>⟩</mo></mrow><mo>]</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mfrac><mn>2</mn><mi>π</mi></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">⟨</mo><mi>𝒚</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">⟩</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left&lt;{\bm{y}},Q_{\tt mse}^{-1}\left(Q_{\tt mse}({\bm{x}})\right)\right&gt;\right]=\frac{2}{\pi}\cdot\langle{\bm{y}},{\bm{x}}\rangle</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝔼[⟨𝒚,Q𝚖𝚜𝚎−1​(Q𝚖𝚜𝚎​(𝒙))⟩]=2π⋅⟨𝒚,𝒙⟩\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left<{\bm{y}},Q_{\tt mse}^{-1}\left(Q_{\tt mse}({\bm{x}})\right)\right>\right]=\frac{2}{\pi}\cdot\langle{\bm{y}},{\bm{x}}\rangle, which has a multiplicative bias of <!-- MathML: <math alttext="2/\pi" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.p1.12.m12" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo>/</mo><mi>π</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">2/\pi</annotation></semantics></math> --> 2/π2/\pi.

To address this bias, we propose a solution that combines <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}" id="S3.SS2.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse} with an instance of QJL [[62](#bib.bib62 "")].
Specifically, let <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="Q_{\tt mse}" display="inline" id="S3.SS2.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚖𝚜𝚎Q_{\tt mse} be the quantization map corresponding to <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}" id="S3.SS2.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse} with a bit-width of <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="b-1" display="inline" id="S3.SS2.p2.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b-1</annotation></semantics></math> --> b−1b-1.
For any <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}" id="S3.SS2.p2.5.m5" alttext="{\bm{r}}:={\bm{x}}-Q_{\tt mse}^{-1}\left(Q_{\tt mse}({\bm{x}})\right)" display="inline" id="S3.SS2.p2.6.m6" We can then apply the QJL quantization map <!-- MathML: <math alttext="Q_{\tt qjl}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.p2.8.m8" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>Q</mi><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">Q_{\tt qjl}</annotation></semantics></math> --> Q𝚚𝚓𝚕Q_{\tt qjl} on this residual vector, resulting in an overall bit-width of <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="b" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.p2.9.m9" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>b</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b</annotation></semantics></math> --> bb and providing the following unbiased inner product estimator:

11: <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\tilde{{\bm{x}}}_{\tt qjl}\leftarrow\frac{\sqrt{\pi/2}}{d}\cdot\gamma\cdot{\bm{S}}^{\top}\cdot{\tt qjl}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="alg2.l11.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mover accent="true"><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>~</mo></mover><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">←</mo><mrow><mfrac><msqrt><mrow><mi>π</mi><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msqrt><mi>d</mi></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>γ</mi><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><msup><mi>𝑺</mi><mo>⊤</mo></msup><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\tilde{{\bm{x}}}_{\tt qjl}\leftarrow\frac{\sqrt{\pi/2}}{d}\cdot\gamma\cdot{\bm{S}}^{\top}\cdot{\tt qjl}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝒙~𝚚𝚓𝚕←π/2d⋅γ⋅𝑺⊤⋅𝚚𝚓𝚕\tilde{{\bm{x}}}_{\tt qjl}\leftarrow\frac{\sqrt{\pi/2}}{d}\cdot\gamma\cdot{\bm{S}}^{\top}\cdot{\tt qjl}

We prove the main result for <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.p3.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod} in the following theorem.

###### Theorem 2 (performance guarantee: <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmtheorem2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}).

For any bit-width <!-- MathML: <math alttext="b\geq 1" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b\geq 1</annotation></semantics></math> --> b≥1b\geq 1 and any vector <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐱</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱∈SSd−1{\bm{x}}\in\SS^{d-1}, the procedure <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{Quant}_{\tt prod}({\bm{x}})" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.3.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">Quant</mtext><mi>𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝐱</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{Quant}_{\tt prod}({\bm{x}})</annotation></semantics></math> --> Quant𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍​(𝐱)\textsc{Quant}_{\tt prod}({\bm{x}}) in ?? outputs an index vector <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="{\tt idx}\in[2^{b-1}]^{d}" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.4.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚒𝚍𝚡</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><msup><mn>2</mn><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\tt idx}\in[2^{b-1}]^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚒𝚍𝚡∈[2b−1]d{\tt idx}\in[2^{b-1}]^{d} along with a sign vector <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\tt qjl}\in\{-1,1\}^{d}" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.5.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo>,</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\tt qjl}\in\{-1,1\}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝚚𝚓𝚕∈{−1,1}d{\tt qjl}\in\{-1,1\}^{d} and a positive number <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\gamma\geq 0" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.6.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>γ</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\gamma\geq 0</annotation></semantics></math> --> γ≥0\gamma\geq 0.
When these vectors and the scalar value are passed to the primitive <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{DeQuant}_{\tt prod}({\tt idx},{\tt qjl},\gamma)" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.7.7.m7" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">DeQuant</mtext><mi>𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝚒𝚍𝚡</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>𝚚𝚓𝚕</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>γ</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{DeQuant}_{\tt prod}({\tt idx},{\tt qjl},\gamma)</annotation></semantics></math> --> DeQuant𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍​(𝚒𝚍𝚡,𝚚𝚓𝚕,γ)\textsc{DeQuant}_{\tt prod}({\tt idx},{\tt qjl},\gamma), it produces a reconstructed vector <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.8.8.m8" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mover accent="true"><mi>𝐱</mi><mo>~</mo></mover><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐱~∈ℝd\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d} that for any vector <!-- MathML: <math alttext="{\bm{y}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem2.p1.9.9.m9" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐲</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>ℝ</mi><mi>d</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{y}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐲∈ℝd{\bm{y}}\in\mathbb{R}^{d} satisfies the following properties:

- •
      Expected inner-product <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}_{\tilde{{\bm{x}}}}\left[\left&lt;{\bm{y}},\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\right&gt;\right]=\langle{\bm{y}},{\bm{x}}\rangle" id="S3.I2.i1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><msub><mo>𝔼</mo><mover accent="true"><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>~</mo></mover></msub><mrow><mo lspace="0em">[</mo><mrow><mo>⟨</mo><mi>𝒚</mi><mo>,</mo><mover accent="true"><mi>𝒙</mi><mo>~</mo></mover><mo>⟩</mo></mrow><mo>]</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">⟨</mo><mi>𝒚</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">⟩</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}_{\tilde{{\bm{x}}}}\left[\left&lt;{\bm{y}},\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\right&gt;\right]=\langle{\bm{y}},{\bm{x}}\rangle</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝔼𝒙~[⟨𝒚,𝒙~⟩]=⟨𝒚,𝒙⟩\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}_{\tilde{{\bm{x}}}}\left[\left<{\bm{y}},\tilde{{\bm{x}}}\right>\right]=\langle{\bm{y}},{\bm{x}}\rangle

- •

###### Proof.

Now we can computed the unconditional expectation using the law of total expectation: <!-- MathML: <math The theorem follows by invoking the MSE bounds from ?? with bit-width <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="b-1" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS2.3.p3.2.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b-1</annotation></semantics></math> --> b−1b-1.
∎

### 3.3 Lower Bounds

We show that TurboQuant achieves an optimal distortion rate, up to a small constant factor, for any bit-width by proving lower bounds on the best achievable distortion for any compression algorithm.
Our lower bound proof leverages Yao’s minimax principle.
This principle allows us to relate the lower bound for randomized algorithms with worst-case deterministic input vectors to the lower bound for deterministic algorithms with randomized input vectors.
Subsequently, we derive a lower bound on the achievable distortion rate for the latter using Shannon’s lower bound (SLB) presented in ??.
Formally, we prove the following theorem.

###### Theorem 3 (lower bound on best achievable compression distortion).

For any randomized quantization algorithm <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="Q:\SS^{d-1}\to\{0,1\}^{b\cdot d}" intent=":literal" id="Thmtheorem3.p1.1.1.m1" Furthermore, there exists a <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="{\bm{y}}\in\SS^{d-1}" display="inline" id="Thmtheorem3.p1.5.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>𝐲</mi><mo>∈</mo><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\bm{y}}\in\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝐲∈SSd−1{\bm{y}}\in\SS^{d-1} such that:

| --- | --- | --- |

###### Proof.

By Yao’s minimax principle the expected MSE of the optimal randomized compression algorithm for worst-case inputs (<!-- MathML: <math alttext="D_{\tt mse}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS3.1.p1.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎D_{\tt mse}) is equal to the expected MSE of the optimal deterministic compression algorithm when applied to inputs drawn from a maximally difficult randomized distribution.
By definition, the MSE of the latter scenario is lower-bounded by the best achievable MSE for inputs uniformly distributed on the unit hypersphere.

The best achievable MSE for a compression algorithm with bit-width <!-- MathML: <math alttext="b" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS3.2.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>b</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b</annotation></semantics></math> --> bb, operating on uniformly distributed inputs from the sphere <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\SS^{d-1}" id="S3.SS3.2.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msup><mi>SS</mi><mrow><mi>d</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\SS^{d-1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> SSd−1\SS^{d-1}, is lower bounded in ??.
Therefore, by invoking ?? we conclude that <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="D_{\tt mse}\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS3.2.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo>≥</mo><mfrac><mn>1</mn><msup><mn>4</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎≥14bD_{\tt mse}\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}.

Furthermore, from <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="D_{\tt mse}\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}" id="S3.SS3.3.p3.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><mo>≥</mo><mfrac><mn>1</mn><msup><mn>4</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎≥14bD_{\tt mse}\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}} and using the definition of <!-- MathML: <math alttext="D_{\tt mse}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS3.3.p3.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎D_{\tt mse} we conclude that:

|  | <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\displaystyle D_{\tt mse}" id="S3.Ex33.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle D_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> D𝚖𝚜𝚎\displaystyle D_{\tt mse} | <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\displaystyle=\sum_{j=1}^{d}\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|{\bm{x}}_{j}-\left[Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\right]_{j}\right|^{2}\right]" id="S3.Ex33.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi /><mo>=</mo><mrow><mstyle displaystyle="true"><munderover><mo movablelimits="false">∑</mo><mrow><mi>j</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mi>d</mi></munderover></mstyle><mrow><mo movablelimits="false" lspace="0.167em" rspace="0em">𝔼</mo><mrow><mo>[</mo><msup><mrow><mo>|</mo><mrow><msub><mi>𝒙</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mo>[</mo><mrow><msup><mi>Q</mi><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mi>Q</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>]</mo></mrow><mi>j</mi></msub></mrow><mo>|</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>]</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle=\sum_{j=1}^{d}\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|{\bm{x}}_{j}-\left[Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\right]_{j}\right|^{2}\right]</annotation></semantics></math> --> =∑j=1d𝔼[|𝒙j−[Q−1​(Q​(𝒙))]j|2]\displaystyle=\sum_{j=1}^{d}\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|{\bm{x}}_{j}-\left[Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\right]_{j}\right|^{2}\right] |  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  |  | <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="\displaystyle=\sum_{j=1}^{d}\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},{\bm{x}}\rangle-\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\rangle\right|^{2}\right]" intent=":literal" id="S3.Ex34.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi /><mo>=</mo><mrow><mstyle displaystyle="true"><munderover><mo movablelimits="false">∑</mo><mrow><mi>j</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mi>d</mi></munderover></mstyle><mrow><mo movablelimits="false" lspace="0.167em" rspace="0em">𝔼</mo><mrow><mo>[</mo><msup><mrow><mo>|</mo><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">⟨</mo><msub><mi>𝒆</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">⟩</mo></mrow><mo>−</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">⟨</mo><msub><mi>𝒆</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><mo>,</mo><mrow><msup><mi>Q</mi><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mi>Q</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="false">⟩</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>|</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>]</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle=\sum_{j=1}^{d}\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},{\bm{x}}\rangle-\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\rangle\right|^{2}\right]</annotation></semantics></math> --> =∑j=1d𝔼[|⟨𝒆j,𝒙⟩−⟨𝒆j,Q−1​(Q​(𝒙))⟩|2]\displaystyle=\sum_{j=1}^{d}\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},{\bm{x}}\rangle-\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\rangle\right|^{2}\right] |  |
|  |  | <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\displaystyle\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}." display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.Ex35.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi /><mo>≥</mo><mstyle displaystyle="true"><mfrac><mn>1</mn><msup><mn>4</mn><mi>b</mi></msup></mfrac></mstyle></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}.</annotation></semantics></math> --> ≥14b.\displaystyle\geq\frac{1}{4^{b}}. |  |


By pigeonhole principle there exist an index <!-- MathML: <math alttext="j\in[d]" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S3.SS3.3.p3.3.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>j</mi><mo>∈</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><mi>d</mi><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation id="S3.SS3.3.p3.4.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mo rspace="0em">𝔼</mo><mrow><mo>[</mo><msup><mrow><mo>|</mo><mrow><mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>𝒙</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="false">⟩</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>|</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>]</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>≥</mo><mrow><mfrac><mn>1</mn><mi>d</mi></mfrac><mo lspace="0.222em" encoding="application/x-tex">\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},{\bm{x}}\rangle-\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\rangle\right|^{2}\right]\geq\frac{1}{d}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝔼[|⟨𝒆j,𝒙⟩−⟨𝒆j,Q−1​(Q​(𝒙))⟩|2]≥1d⋅14b\mathop{{\mathbb{E}}}\left[\left|\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},{\bm{x}}\rangle-\langle{\bm{e}}_{j},Q^{-1}\left(Q({\bm{x}})\right)\rangle\right|^{2}\right]\geq\frac{1}{d}\cdot\frac{1}{4^{b}}, which completes the proof.
∎

We note that a comparable lower bound for the *worst-case* distortion in vector quantization can be derived using “sphere packing” arguments (indeed, with larger constants as this is a harder problem) [[26](#bib.bib26 "")].
However, ?? offers a more robust and relevant lower bound for our analysis.
This is because it establishes a lower bound on the *expected distortion*, rather than the worst-case error, and aligns seamlessly with our upper bounds presented in ?? and ??.

## 4 Experiments

All experiments are performed using a single NVIDIA A100 GPU.
The experimental section is divided into two parts: one to empirically validate the theoretical results, and another to evaluate the performance of our methods on downstream tasks, specifically KV cache quantization and nearest neighbor vector search.

### 4.1 Empirical Validation

(a) <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}" display="inline" id="S4.F1.sf1.2.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext mathsize="0.900em" class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext mathsize="0.900em" class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">prod</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantprod\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x1.png)

(b) <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}" id="S4.F1.sf2.2.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext mathsize="0.900em" class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext mathsize="0.900em" class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">mse</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantmse\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x2.png)

Figure 1: Error distribution of <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}" id="S4.F1.3.3.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">prod</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantprod\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}} and <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}" id="S4.F1.4.4.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">mse</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantmse\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}} for Inner Product Estimation.

In this section, we verify the theoretical results established in previous sections. We conduct our experiments using the DBpedia Entities dataset, which has been encoded into a 1536-dimensional space using OpenAI3 embeddings.
To perform our experiments, we randomly sample 100,000 data points from the dataset, denoted as training set, which serves as our primary dataset. Additionally, we extract 1,000 distinct entries, denoted as query set, to be used as query points.

We evaluate two quantization methods: <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS1.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">prod</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantprod\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}} and <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS1.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">mse</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantmse\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}. The method <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S4.SS1.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">mse</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantmse\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}} is designed to be optimzed for estimating the mean squared error (MSE) between the quantized and original vectors. In contrast, <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}" id="S4.SS1.p2.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">prod</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantprod\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}} is unbiased for estimating the inner product between the quantized and original vectors.

(a) <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}" id="S4.F2.sf1.2.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext mathsize="0.900em" class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi mathsize="0.900em">𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x3.png)

(b) <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S4.F2.sf2.2.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext mathsize="0.900em" class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi mathsize="0.900em">𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x4.png)

Figure 2: The variance of Inner-product error remains constant for <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}" id="S4.F2.4.4.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚙𝚛𝚘𝚍\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt prod}, while in <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}" display="inline" id="S4.F2.5.5.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mi>𝚖𝚜𝚎</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuant𝚖𝚜𝚎\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\tt mse} increases with the average inner product. Bit-width is <!-- MathML: <math alttext="b=2" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.F2.6.6.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b=2</annotation></semantics></math> --> b=2b=2.

Both methods are applied to the task of inner product estimation by quantizing training set and analyzing the distortion in inner product calculations across different bit widths. As shown in ??, increasing the bit width reduces variance in both methods. However, when used for inner product estimation, <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS1.p3.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">mse</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantmse\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}} introduces bias. This bias diminishes as the bit width increases and eventually converges to zero.

The experimental results, illustrated in ??, confirm that <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}" display="inline" id="S4.SS1.p4.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">prod</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantprod\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt prod}} remains unbiased for inner product estimation across all bit widths, while <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S4.SS1.p4.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><msub><mtext class="ltx_font_smallcaps">TurboQuant</mtext><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">mse</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}}</annotation></semantics></math> --> TurboQuantmse\textsc{TurboQuant}_{\text{\tt mse}} gradually improves with increasing bit width.

As observed in ??, when quantizing to 2 bits, the variance remains constant regardless of the inner product of the original vector in the TurboQuantprod approach. However, the same plot indicates that the bias in the TurboQuantmse approach is dependent on the average inner product. As the average inner product increases, the bias also increases.

(a) inner-prod error![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x5.png)

(b) MSE![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x6.png)

Figure 3: Comparison of inner-product error and MSE against theoretical bounds across different bit ratios.

Along with the histograms, we also plot ?? the average inner product error and MSE between the original and quantized vectors across different bit ratios. These plots are drawn alongside the upper and lower bounds established in our theoretical analysis. Our observations confirm that the results align with the theoretical predictions. Specifically, for inner product estimation, the TurboQuantprod approach performs better at lower bit ratios. However, as the bit count increases, TurboQuantmse reduces bias and ultimately achieves superior performance in inner product estimation.

### 4.2 Needle-In-A-Haystack

| SnapKV Score: 0.858![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x7.png) | PyramidKV Score: 0.895![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x8.png) | KIVI Score: 0.981![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x9.png) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| PolarQuant Score: 0.995![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x10.png) | Full-Precision Score: 0.997![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x11.png) | TurboQuant Score: 0.997![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x12.png) |Figure 4: Evaluation of Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct on the “Needle-In-A-Haystack” test, where a model must retrieve a hidden sentence from long-context sequences. While some methods struggle with recall, TurboQuant, despite being more than <!-- MathML: <math alttext="4\times" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.F4.8.1.m1" class="ltx_math_unparsed"><semantics><mrow><mn>4</mn><mo lspace="0.222em">×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">4\times</annotation></semantics></math> --> 4×4\times quantized, achieves the same exact performance as the uncompressed baseline.

The “Needle-In-A-Haystack Test”” [[32](#bib.bib32 "")] is a benchmark designed to evaluate a model’s ability to retrieve specific information embedded within a long document. The test involves placing a unique sentence (the ”needle”) at an arbitrary location within a much larger text (the ”haystack”) and assessing whether the model can successfully extract it.

Following the experimental setup of Fu et al. [[21](#bib.bib21 "")], we conduct evaluations using the <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{Llama}" display="inline" id="S4.SS2.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙻𝚕𝚊𝚖𝚊</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Llama}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙻𝚕𝚊𝚖𝚊\mathtt{Llama}-<!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathtt{3.1}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS2.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mn mathvariant="monospace" class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">3.1</mn><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{3.1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 3.1\mathtt{3.1}-<!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathtt{8B}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS2.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>𝟾</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>𝙱</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{8B}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝟾​𝙱\mathtt{8B}-<!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{Instruct}" id="S4.SS2.p2.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Instruct}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝\mathtt{Instruct} model. To analyze performance across different input sequence lengths, we vary the document size from *4k to 104k tokens*. The primary metric used for evaluation is the *recall score*, which measures how accurately the model retrieves the hidden sentence.

For comparison, we benchmark our approach against several state-of-the-art memory-efficient methods, including PolarQuant [[28](#bib.bib28 "")], SnapKV [[38](#bib.bib38 "")], PyramidKV [[12](#bib.bib12 "")], and KIVI [[41](#bib.bib41 "")]. Each method is tested under a memory compression ratio of 0.25, meaning that only 25% of the full KV cache is utilized.

The results, illustrated in ??, reveal that quantization methods with theoretical guarantees, such as PolarQuant and TurboQuant, outperform token-level compression techniques like SnapKV and PyramidKV, as well as scalar quantization approaches like KIVI, which lack formal theoretical guarantees. Notably, TurboQuant achieves identical performance to the full-precision model, even at <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="4\times" display="inline" id="S4.SS2.p4.1.m1" class="ltx_math_unparsed"><semantics><mrow><mn>4</mn><mo lspace="0.222em">×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">4\times</annotation></semantics></math> --> 4×4\times compression, making it a robust solution for long-context processing.

### 4.3 End-to-end Generation on LongBench

We experiment with various KV cache compression algorithms on the LongBench dataset [[10](#bib.bib10 "")], which encompasses a broad range of long-text scenarios, including single- and multi-document question-answering, summarization, few-shot learning, synthetic tasks, and code completion. To ensure a balanced evaluation across different context lengths, we employ LongBench-E, a subset designed with a more uniform length distribution. This enables a fair assessment of each model’s performance across varying context sizes, making it a more reliable benchmark for evaluating compression techniques.

We compare TurboQuant against the leading baseline methods introduced in ??, using both <!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathtt{Llama}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS3.p2.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙻𝚕𝚊𝚖𝚊</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Llama}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙻𝚕𝚊𝚖𝚊\mathtt{Llama}-<!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{3.1}" id="S4.SS3.p2.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mn mathvariant="monospace" class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">3.1</mn><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{3.1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 3.1\mathtt{3.1}-<!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathtt{8B}" display="inline" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS3.p2.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>𝟾</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>𝙱</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{8B}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝟾​𝙱\mathtt{8B}-<!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{Instruct}" display="inline" id="S4.SS3.p2.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Instruct}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝\mathtt{Instruct} and <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\mathtt{Ministral}" id="S4.SS3.p2.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙼𝚒𝚗𝚒𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚊𝚕</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Ministral}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙼𝚒𝚗𝚒𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚊𝚕\mathtt{Ministral}-<!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathtt{7B}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S4.SS3.p2.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>𝟽</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>𝙱</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{7B}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝟽​𝙱\mathtt{7B}-<!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{Instruct}" display="inline" id="S4.SS3.p2.7.m7" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Instruct}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝\mathtt{Instruct}. Unlike existing approaches such as KIVI and PolarQuant, which leave generated tokens unquantized, our method applies quantization even during the streaming generation process.

As shown in ??, our approach outperforms other methods for both <!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{Llama}" display="inline" id="S4.SS3.p3.1.m1" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙻𝚕𝚊𝚖𝚊</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Llama}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙻𝚕𝚊𝚖𝚊\mathtt{Llama}-<!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{3.1}" id="S4.SS3.p3.2.m2" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mn mathvariant="monospace" class="ltx_mathvariant_monospace">3.1</mn><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{3.1}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 3.1\mathtt{3.1}-<!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="\mathtt{8B}" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS3.p3.3.m3" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>𝟾</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>𝙱</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{8B}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝟾​𝙱\mathtt{8B}-<!-- MathML: <math alttext="\mathtt{Instruct}" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S4.SS3.p3.4.m4" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Instruct}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝\mathtt{Instruct} and <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" alttext="\mathtt{Ministral}" intent=":literal" id="S4.SS3.p3.5.m5" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙼𝚒𝚗𝚒𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚊𝚕</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Ministral}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙼𝚒𝚗𝚒𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚊𝚕\mathtt{Ministral}-<!-- MathML: <math intent=":literal" display="inline" alttext="\mathtt{7B}" id="S4.SS3.p3.6.m6" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mn>𝟽</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>𝙱</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{7B}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝟽​𝙱\mathtt{7B}-<!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="\mathtt{Instruct}" id="S4.SS3.p3.7.m7" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mi>𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathtt{Instruct}</annotation></semantics></math> --> 𝙸𝚗𝚜𝚝𝚛𝚞𝚌𝚝\mathtt{Instruct}, achieving significantly higher average scores. We evaluate our method using 2.5-bit and 3.5-bit quantization during text generation.
These non-integer bit precisions result from our strategy of splitting channels into outlier and non-outlier sets, and applying two independent instances of TurboQuant to each, allocating higher bit precision to outliers. This outlier treatment strategy is consistent with prior work [[63](#bib.bib63 ""), [51](#bib.bib51 "")] .
For example, in our 2.5-bit setup, 32 outlier channels are quantized at 3 bits, while the remaining 96 channels use 2 bits, leading to an effective bit precision of <!-- MathML: <math alttext="(32\times 3+96\times 2)/128=2.5" intent=":literal" display="inline" id="S4.SS3.p3.8.m8" class="ltx_Math"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mrow><mn>32</mn><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">×</mo><mn>3</mn></mrow><mo>+</mo><mrow><mn>96</mn><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0.222em">×</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>/</mo><mn>128</mn></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>2.5</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(32\times 3+96\times 2)/128=2.5</annotation></semantics></math> --> (32×3+96×2)/128=2.5(32\times 3+96\times 2)/128=2.5. For 3.5-bit quantization, a different ratio of outliers and regular channels leads to a higher effective bit precision.
Despite using fewer bits than competing techniques, TurboQuant maintains performance comparable to unquantized models. Remarkably, we achieve this while compressing quantized vectors by at least a factor of <!-- MathML: <math display="inline" intent=":literal" alttext="4.5\times" id="S4.SS3.p3.9.m9" class="ltx_math_unparsed"><semantics><mrow><mn>4.5</mn><mo lspace="0.222em">×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">4.5\times</annotation></semantics></math> --> 4.5×4.5\times.

### 4.4 Near Neighbour Search Experiments

In this section, we establish the strength of our proposed method, even in the context of near-neighbor search. We conduct our experiments using the DBpedia [[53](#bib.bib53 "")] Entities dataset, which has been encoded into 1536-dimensional111https://huggingface.co/datasets/Qdrant/dbpedia-entities-openai3-text-embedding-3-large-1536-1M and 3072-dimensional 222https://huggingface.co/datasets/Qdrant/dbpedia-entities-openai3-text-embedding-3-large-3072-1M spaces using OpenAI3 embeddings. Additionally, we evaluate performance on a lower-dimensional dataset, utilizing the standard GloVe [[45](#bib.bib45 "")] embeddings.
To construct our experimental setup, we randomly sample 100,000 data points from the dataset, denoted as training set, which serves as our primary training and evaluation set. Furthermore, we extract 1,000 distinct entries, denoted as query set, to be used as query points for datasets that do not explicitly provide a query set. For the GloVe dataset, we use a pre-existing query set consisting of 10,000 points.

We compare our method, TurboQuant, against two baseline quantization approaches: Product Quantization (PQ) and RabitQ [[22](#bib.bib22 "")]. To ensure a fair comparison, we quantize the dataset training set using all three methods and evaluate their performance based on recall ratio at top-k, denoted as 1@k. Specifically, this metric assesses how often the true top inner product result is captured within the top-k approximated results returned by each algorithm.

| Approach | d=200 | d=1536 | d=3072 |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Product Quantization | 37.04 | 239.75 | 494.42 |
| RabitQ | 597.25 | 2267.59 | 3957.19 |
| TurboQuant | 0.0007 | 0.0013 | 0.0021 |Table 2: Quantization time (in seconds) for different approaches across various dimensions using 4-bit quantization.

Product Quantization (PQ) relies on the k-means algorithm to construct codebooks, which require separate storage.
As the number of bits increases, the size of the codebook grows exponentially, leading to additional storage overhead. In our experiments, we carefully tuned the parameters to match the bit allocation of other methods.
The most efficient implementation, designed for rapid querying, employs AVX2 In-Register Lookup Tables (LUTs).
Specifically, it uses LUT16 with (l = 16) codewords. However, we observed substantial quality degradation at this configuration.
To achieve a balance between speed and accuracy, we opted for a version of PQ that uses LUT256, which contains 256 codewords.
For 2-bit quantization, it groups 4 coordinates per lookup, while for 4-bit quantization, it groups 2 coordinates per lookup.
Notably, since we use the same dataset for both training and evaluation, PQ benefits from an inherent advantage in this setup.

RabitQ. Unlike PQ, RabitQ lacks a fully vectorized implementation, making it impossible to leverage GPU acceleration. As a result, it runs significantly slower on CPU. Additionally, the method incurs extra computational overheads that we do not explicitly account for in the bit ratio comparisons. While RabitQ claims a certain bit ratio, in practice, it utilizes more bits than reported due to these inefficiencies.

Despite the advantages granted to the baseline methods, TurboQuant consistently outperforms both Product Quantization and RabitQ in terms of recall ratio across all experiments. This demonstrates the robustness and efficiency of our approach, making it a compelling alternative for high-dimensional quantization-based search tasks.

(a) GloVe - d=200![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x13.png)

(b) OpenAI3 - d=1536![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x14.png)

(c) OpenAI3 - d=3072![Refer to caption](2504.19874v1/x15.png)

Figure 5: Recall comparison on different datasets with different embedding dimensions.

## Instructions for reporting errors

We are continuing to improve HTML versions of papers, and your feedback helps enhance accessibility and mobile
 support. To report errors in the HTML that will help us improve conversion and rendering, choose any of the
 methods listed below:

- Click the "Report Issue" (![SVG Image](data:image/svg+xml;base64,PHN2ZyBzdHlsZT0iZGlzcGxheTogaW5saW5lLWJsb2NrOyB2ZXJ0aWNhbC1hbGlnbjogbWlkZGxlOyBmaWxsOiB2YXIoLS10ZXh0LWNvbG9yKTsiIHJvbGU9InByZXNlbnRhdGlvbiIgYXJpYS1oaWRkZW49InRydWUiIGhlaWdodD0iMWVtIiB2aWV3Qm94PSIwIDAgNjQwIDY0MCI+CiAgICAgICAgICAgIDxwYXRoIGQ9Ik0yMjQgMTYwQzIyNCAxMDcgMjY3IDY0IDMyMCA2NEMzNzMgNjQgNDE2IDEwNyA0MTYgMTYwTDQxNiAxNjMuNkM0MTYgMTc5LjMgNDAzLjMgMTkyIDM4Ny42IDE5MkwyNTIuNSAxOTJDMjM2LjggMTkyIDIyNC4xIDE3OS4zIDIyNC4xIDE2My42TDIyNC4xIDE2MHpNNTY5LjYgMTcyLjhDNTgwLjIgMTg2LjkgNTc3LjMgMjA3IDU2My4yIDIxNy42TDQ2NS40IDI5MC45QzQ3MC43IDI5OS44IDQ3NC43IDMwOS42IDQ3Ny4yIDMyMEw1NzYgMzIwQzU5My43IDMyMCA2MDggMzM0LjMgNjA4IDM1MkM2MDggMzY5LjcgNTkzLjcgMzg0IDU3NiAzODRMNDgwIDM4NEw0ODAgNDE2QzQ4MCA0MTguNiA0NzkuOSA0MjEuMyA0NzkuOCA0MjMuOUw1NjMuMiA0ODYuNEM1NzcuMyA0OTcgNTgwLjIgNTE3LjEgNTY5LjYgNTMxLjJDNTU5IDU0NS4zIDUzOC45IDU0OC4yIDUyNC44IDUzNy42TDQ2MS43IDQ5MC4zQzQzOC41IDUzNC41IDM5NS4yIDU2Ni41IDM0NCA1NzQuMkwzNDQgMzQ0QzM0NCAzMzAuNyAzMzMuMyAzMjAgMzIwIDMyMEMzMDYuNyAzMjAgMjk2IDMzMC43IDI5NiAzNDRMMjk2IDU3NC4yQzI0NC44IDU2Ni41IDIwMS41IDUzNC41IDE3OC4zIDQ5MC4zTDExNS4yIDUzNy42QzEwMS4xIDU0OC4yIDgxIDU0NS4zIDcwLjQgNTMxLjJDNTkuOCA1MTcuMSA2Mi43IDQ5NyA3Ni44IDQ4Ni40TDE2MC4yIDQyMy45QzE2MC4xIDQyMS4zIDE2MCA0MTguNyAxNjAgNDE2TDE2MCAzODRMNjQgMzg0QzQ2LjMgMzg0IDMyIDM2OS43IDMyIDM1MkMzMiAzMzQuMyA0Ni4zIDMyMCA2NCAzMjBMMTYyLjggMzIwQzE2NS4zIDMwOS42IDE2OS4zIDI5OS44IDE3NC42IDI5MC45TDc2LjggMjE3LjZDNjIuNyAyMDcgNTkuOCAxODYuOSA3MC40IDE3Mi44QzgxIDE1OC43IDEwMS4xIDE1NS44IDExNS4yIDE2Ni40TDIyNCAyNDhDMjM2LjMgMjQyLjkgMjQ5LjggMjQwIDI2NCAyNDBMMzc2IDI0MEMzOTAuMiAyNDAgNDAzLjcgMjQyLjggNDE2IDI0OEw1MjQuOCAxNjYuNEM1MzguOSAxNTUuOCA1NTkgMTU4LjcgNTY5LjYgMTcyLjh6IiAvPgogICAgICAgICAgPC9zdmc+)) button, located in the page header.


**Tip:** You can select the relevant text first, to include it in your report.

Our team has already identified [the following issues](https://github.com/arXiv/html_feedback/issues). We appreciate your time reviewing and reporting rendering errors we
 may not have found yet. Your efforts will help us improve the HTML versions for all readers, because disability
 should not be a barrier to accessing research. Thank you for your continued support in championing open access for
 all.

Have a free development cycle? Help support accessibility at arXiv! Our collaborators at LaTeXML maintain a [list of packages that need conversion](https://github.com/brucemiller/LaTeXML/wiki/Porting-LaTeX-packages-for-LaTeXML), and welcome [developer contributions](https://github.com/brucemiller/LaTeXML/issues).
